First, because of Japan’s low interest rates, the yen is often used in the so-called “carry trade.” That’s when investors borrow at a low interest rate to invest in an asset with a higher return. \n\nFor example, a fund manager might borrow yen and then invest that money in Indian bonds that offer a higher yield, pocketing the difference. It’s slightly more complex than this in practice, as the fund manager in question would also need to swap rupees for dollars, and hedge his dollar risk, but that’s the general idea. \n\nAll of this means that when the interest-rate differentials between Japan and other major developed nations are high, traders will rush in to borrow yen for the carry trade, which weighs on the currency. And even after rising on Monday, perhaps due to an undisclosed intervention from the Bank of Japan, the dollar is now worth more than 157 Japanese yen, compared to just 129 in January of 2023. There are several reasons for the yen’s underperformance compared to the dollar, but the main one is clear: rising U.S. interest rates. \n\n“The key lesson from Japan’s travails with the yen over the past couple years is that what matters most to the currency’s fortunes is expected interest rate differentials,” Jonas Goltermann, deputy chief market economist at Capital Economics, explained in a Monday note. \n\nThe phrase “expected interest rate differentials” may sound confusing, but it just means the anticipated difference between interest rates in Japan and other parts of the world, particularly the U.S.\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/2024/04/30/japan-currency-yen-cratering-stock-market-interest-rate-differentials-inflation/\n\nSource 2:\nWhen the U.S. or other western powers’ interest rates are higher than Japan’s, it puts pressure on the yen. Goltermann noted there are two main reasons for this phenomenon. First, because of Japan’s low interest rates, the yen is often used in the so-called “carry trade.” That’s when investors borrow at a low interest rate to invest in an asset with a higher return. \n\nFor example, a fund manager might borrow yen and then invest that money in Indian bonds that offer a higher yield, pocketing the difference. It’s slightly more complex than this in practice, as the fund manager in question would also need to swap rupees for dollars, and hedge his dollar risk, but that’s the general idea. \n\nAll of this means that when the interest-rate differentials between Japan and other major developed nations are high, traders will rush in to borrow yen for the carry trade, which weighs on the currency. And Bank of America’s analysts warned in a Friday note that the carry trade “is unlikely to start diminishing meaningfully until the Fed starts cutting rates, which our U.S. economists expect in December.”\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/2024/04/30/japan-currency-yen-cratering-stock-market-interest-rate-differentials-inflation/\n\nSource 3:\nThe Bank of Japan increased interest rates in mid-March to 0-0.1%, ending the last remaining negative interest rate regime among major economies. In a conversation with reporters, Japan Airlines CEO Mitsuko Tottori suggested <https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2024-04-24/japan-airlines-ceo-tottori-describes-weak-yen-as-a-big-problem?sref=nfBouUGg> that business leaders would be more comfortable with an exchange rate of around 130 yen to the U.S. dollar. \n\nJapanese officials have said they’ll step in to support the yen if the currency depreciates too quickly. Japan intervened in foreign exchange markets three times in 2022 to prop up its currency. \n\nLast week, Japan, South Korea, and the U.S. released a statement that acknowledged the two Asian countries’ “serious concerns” about the “recent sharp depreciation” of their currencies. \n\nThis story was originally featured on Fortune.com <https://fortune.com/asia/2024/04/25/fed-central-bankers-southeast-asia-time-rate-cuts-ensure-currencies-no-collapse/>\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/asia/2024/04/25/fed-central-bankers-southeast-asia-time-rate-cuts-ensure-currencies-no-collapse/\n\nSource 5:\nIn March, Japan’s central bank hiked interest rates <https://fortune.com/asia/2024/03/19/japan-central-bank-hiked-benchmark-interest-rate-first-time-17-years-scrapping-world-last-negative/> above zero for the first time in 17 years. The rate hike came after Japanese companies agreed to large pay increases, giving hope that Japanese consumers might start spending more and boost the economy. \n\nJapanese companies typically viewed a weak yen as a good thing as it lowers the cost of exports and increases the value of repatriated overseas profits. But the yen may now be too weak for corporate Japan’s liking, as expensive imports eat away at margins and depress consumer spending. \n\nEven Japan Airlines—a company that relies on travel for its revenue—is growing wary  <https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2024-04-24/japan-airlines-ceo-tottori-describes-weak-yen-as-a-big-problem>of the weak yen, as outbound travel becomes prohibitively expensive for many Japanese.\n\nWhy is Japan complaining about overtourism?\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/asia/2024/07/10/japan-travel-weak-yen-instagram-culture-rakuten-overtourism/\n\nSource 5:\nVisitors now want unique social media-ready experiences rather than just food and shopping. “It’s the Instagram culture, right?” Bek says. “It’s not about what you eat or what you do. A year ago, one U.S. dollar was worth 140 yen; it\'s worth around 161 yen now. \n\nThe U.S. Federal Reserve’s “higher-for-longer” stance on rates makes the U.S. dollar more attractive to investors and puts pressure on many Asian currencies.\n\nJapan resisted raising its interest rates as it fought a decades-long battle against deflation. The Bank of Japan kept its key interest rate in negative territory even as other central banks tried to match U.S. central bank\'s moves during the hiking cycle between 2022 and mid-2023.\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/asia/2024/07/10/japan-travel-weak-yen-instagram-culture-rakuten-overtourism/\n\nSource 6:\nKyle Rodda was on the 34th floor of the Capital.com office in Melbourne when the yen suddenly plunged to around 160 per dollar early in the Asia day. In a conversation with reporters, Japan Airlines CEO Mitsuko Tottori suggested <https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2024-04-24/japan-airlines-ceo-tottori-describes-weak-yen-as-a-big-problem?sref=nfBouUGg> that business leaders would be more comfortable with an exchange rate of around 130 yen to the U.S. dollar. \n\nJapanese officials have said they’ll step in to support the yen if the currency depreciates too quickly. Japan intervened in foreign exchange markets three times in 2022 to prop up its currency. \n\nLast week, Japan, South Korea, and the U.S. released a statement that acknowledged the two Asian countries’ “serious concerns” about the “recent sharp depreciation” of their currencies. \n\nThis story was originally featured on Fortune.com <https://fortune.com/asia/2024/04/25/fed-central-bankers-southeast-asia-time-rate-cuts-ensure-currencies-no-collapse/>\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/asia/2024/04/25/fed-central-bankers-southeast-asia-time-rate-cuts-ensure-currencies-no-collapse/\n\nSource 5:\nIn March, Japan’s central bank hiked interest rates <https://fortune.com/asia/2024/03/19/japan-central-bank-hiked-benchmark-interest-rate-first-time-17-years-scrapping-world-last-negative/> above zero for the first time in 17 years. The rate hike came after Japanese companies agreed to large pay increases, giving hope that Japanese consumers might start spending more and boost the economy. \n\nJapanese companies typically viewed a weak yen as a good thing as it lowers the cost of exports and increases the value of repatriated overseas profits. But the yen may now be too weak for corporate Japan’s liking, as expensive imports eat away at margins and depress consumer spending. \n\nEven Japan Airlines—a company that relies on travel for its revenue—is growing wary  <https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2024-04-24/japan-airlines-ceo-tottori-describes-weak-yen-as-a-big-problem>of the weak yen, as outbound travel becomes prohibitively expensive for many Japanese.\n\nWhy is Japan complaining about overtourism?\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/asia/2024/07/10/japan-travel-weak-yen-instagram-culture-rakuten-overtourism/\n\nSource 5:\nVisitors now want unique social media-ready experiences rather than just food and shopping. “It’s the Instagram culture, right?” Bek says. “It’s not about what you eat or what you do. Its luxury market is worth $41.1 billion, according to consumer trend platform Euromonitor <https://www.euromonitor.com/luxury-goods-in-japan/report#:~:text=The%20outlook%20for%20luxury%20goods,in%20current%20value%20terms%20(0.>, and is forecasted to reach $42.3 billion in two years.\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/2024/08/01/luxury-japan-tourism-lvmh-weak-yen-china/\n\nSource 1:\nFor some Chinese shoppers, the 16,700 yuan, or $2,300, price tag <https://www.cnbc.com/2024/07/26/chinese-shoppers-flock-to-japan-to-take-advantage-of-the-weak-yen.html#:~:text=The%20Oliver%20Wyman%20research%20found,when%20compared%20with%20Hong%20Kong.> of a Louis Vuitton Speedy Bandouliere 20 handbag is too much. It’s one reason why more and more are traveling to Japan—where the price for the same bag can be more than $400 lower. \n\nTourists, many of them from China, can thank the weak Japanese yen for the built-in discounts on luxury goods—which are driving surges of visitors to the country for the primary purpose of shopping.\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/2024/08/01/luxury-japan-tourism-lvmh-weak-yen-china/\n\nSource 2:\nJapan’s stock market has been on fire over the past few years. The Nikkei 225, which tracks the 225 largest firms by market cap in Japan’s Tokyo Stock Exchange, has surged 130% from its COVID-induced low in March 2020 to a record high of over 38,000. That’s even better than the impressive 121% surge from the U.S.’s equivalent index, the S&P 500, over the same period.\n\nJapanese stocks’ recent run is quite the shift from the pain of the previous three decades. The Nikkei 225 last hit a record high during a market bubble in 1989, before three “lost decades <https://fortune.com/asia/2024/02/23/why-japan-economy-recession-stock-market-record/>” of low economic growth and stagnant prices in Japan led to years of underperformance. Now, though, just as Japan’s stock market is entering a new era of strength, its currency has collapsed.\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/2024/04/30/japan-currency-yen-cratering-stock-market-interest-rate-differentials-inflation/\n\nSource 2:\nInflation rose to a peak of just 4.3% in Japan in January 2023, and for a nation that has battled deflation for so long, that wasn’t really a big concern for the Bank of Japan, so officials have been slow to raise rates. The Bank of Japan also indicated <https://www.cnbc.com/2024/04/26/bank-of-japan-keeps-monetary-policy-unchanged.html#:~:text=Bank%20of%20Japan-,Bank%20of%20Japan%20keeps%20monetary%20policy%20unchanged%2C%20sees,higher%20inflation%20in%20fiscal%202024&text=The%20Bank%20of%20Japan%20kept,rate%20at%200%25%2D0.1%25.> last week at a policy meeting that it would keep interest rates unchanged, despite the sizable interest rate differential with the U.S. and its declining currency. Although officials added they expect to gradually raise interest rates to 1%, investors balked at their dovish tone oerall, leading the yen to fall. “Markets seem to have reacted more to\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/2024/04/30/japan-currency-yen-cratering-stock-market-interest-rate-differentials-inflation/\n\nSource 2:\nThe yen briefly touched a 34-year low compared to the U.S. dollar this week. And even after rising on Monday, perhaps due to an undisclosed intervention from the Bank of Japan, the dollar is now worth more than 157 Japanese yen, compared to just 129 in January of 2023. There are several reasons for the yen’s underperformance compared to the dollar, but the main one is clear: rising U.S. interest rates. \n\n“The key lesson from Japan’s travails with the yen over the past couple years is that what matters most to the currency’s fortunes is expected interest rate differentials,” Jonas Goltermann, deputy chief market economist at Capital Economics, explained in a Monday note. \n\nThe phrase “expected interest rate differentials” may sound confusing, but it just means the anticipated difference between interest rates in Japan and other parts of the world, particularly the U.S.\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/2024/04/30/japan-currency-yen-cratering-stock-market-interest-rate-differentials-inflation/\n\nSource 2:\nWhen the U.S. or other western powers’ interest rates are higher than Japan’s, it puts pressure on the yen. Goltermann noted there are two main reasons for this phenomenon. First, because of Japan’s low interest rates, the yen is often used in the so-called “carry trade.” That’s when investors borrow at a low interest rate to invest in an asset with a higher return. \n\nFor example, a fund manager might borrow yen and then invest that money in Indian bonds that offer a higher yield, pocketing the difference. It’s slightly more complex than this in practice, as the fund manager in question would also need to swap rupees for dollars, and hedge his dollar risk, but that’s the general idea. \n\nAll of this means that when the interest-rate differentials between Japan and other major developed nations are high, traders will rush in to borrow yen for the carry trade, which weighs on the currency. And Bank of America’s analysts warned in a Friday note that the carry trade “is unlikely to start diminishing meaningfully until the Fed starts cutting rates, which our U.S. economists expect in December.”\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/2024/04/30/japan-currency-yen-cratering-stock-market-interest-rate-differentials-inflation/\n\nSource 3:\nThe Bank of Japan increased interest rates in mid-March to 0-0.1%, ending the last remaining negative interest rate regime among major economies. Central bankers credited <https://fortune.com/asia/2024/04/01/negative-interest-rates-30-year-slump-economists-optimistic-japan-back-data/> successful wage negotiations, where Japanese unions won their biggest pay increases <https://www.reuters.com/markets/asia/japan-unions-will-unveil-results-wage-talks-presaging-shift-central-bank-2024-03-15> in over 30 years, for their decision to hike rates. \n\nIf Japan decides to hold off raising interest rates, that could create another problem <https://fortune.com/2024/04/30/japan-currency-yen-cratering-stock-market-interest-rate-differentials-inflation/>: a weak yen. \n\nThe Japanese yen has fallen to record low as the country\'s interest rates remain much lower than those offered by the U.S. A weak currency makes exports cheaper and imports more expensive; that\'s good for export-focused sectors like tourism and car manufacturing, but hurts households and industries that rely on imported inputs.\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/asia/2024/05/16/japan-escaped-deflation-now-confronts-stagflation-risk-weak-gdp-growth-inflation-interest-rates/\n\nSource 4:\nBut firms are now warning that the yen might be too weak, sending input prices higher and worsening inflation in the country. In a conversation with reporters, Japan Airlines CEO Mitsuko Tottori suggested <https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2024-04-24/japan-airlines-ceo-tottori-describes-weak-yen-as-a-big-problem?sref=nfBouUGg> that business leaders would be more comfortable with an exchange rate of around 130 yen to the U.S. dollar. \n\nJapanese officials have said they’ll step in to support the yen if the currency depreciates too quickly. Japan intervened in foreign exchange markets three times in 2022 to prop up its currency. \n\nLast week, Japan, South Korea, and the U.S. released a statement that acknowledged the two Asian countries’ “serious concerns” about the “recent sharp depreciation” of their currencies. \n\nThis story was originally featured on Fortune.com <https://fortune.com/asia/2024/04/25/fed-central-bankers-southeast-asia-time-rate-cuts-ensure-currencies-no-collapse/>\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/asia/2024/04/25/fed-central-bankers-southeast-asia-time-rate-cuts-ensure-currencies-no-collapse/\n\nSource 5:\nIn March, Japan’s central bank hiked interest rates <https://fortune.com/asia/2024/03/19/japan-central-bank-hiked-benchmark-interest-rate-first-time-17-years-scrapping-world-last-negative/> above zero for the first time in 17 years. The rate hike came after Japanese companies agreed to large pay increases, giving hope that Japanese consumers might start spending more and boost the economy. \n\nJapanese companies typically viewed a weak yen as a good thing as it lowers the cost of exports and increases the value of repatriated overseas profits. But the yen may now be too weak for corporate Japan’s liking, as expensive imports eat away at margins and depress consumer spending. \n\nEven Japan Airlines—a company that relies on travel for its revenue—is growing wary  <https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2024-04-24/japan-airlines-ceo-tottori-describes-weak-yen-as-a-big-problem>of the weak yen, as outbound travel becomes prohibitively expensive for many Japanese.\n\nWhy is Japan complaining about overtourism?\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/asia/2024/07/10/japan-travel-weak-yen-instagram-culture-rakuten-overtourism/\n\nSource 5:\nVisitors now want unique social media-ready experiences rather than just food and shopping. “It’s the Instagram culture, right?” Bek says. “It’s not about what you eat or what you do. It’s about what people see you eat and do. And Japan is beautiful. There’s so many beautiful things to put on Instagram.”\n\nHow is a weak yen helping tourism?\n\nThe yen, long a safe haven for investors during times of crisis, has declined against the dollar. A year ago, one U.S. dollar was worth 140 yen; it\'s worth around 161 yen now. \n\nThe U.S. Federal Reserve’s “higher-for-longer” stance on rates makes the U.S. dollar more attractive to investors and puts pressure on many Asian currencies.\n\nJapan resisted raising its interest rates as it fought a decades-long battle against deflation. The Bank of Japan kept its key interest rate in negative territory even as other central banks tried to match U.S. central bank\'s moves during the hiking cycle between 2022 and mid-2023.\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/asia/2024/07/10/japan-travel-weak-yen-instagram-culture-rakuten-overtourism/\n\nSource 6:\nKyle Rodda was on the 34th floor of the Capital.com office in Melbourne when the yen suddenly plunged to around 160 per dollar early in the Asia day. The trading floor exploded. \n\n“It was much like a game of bingo — everyone shouting 160 all of a sudden,” said Rodda, a strategist who was sitting among six dealers who saw yen trading volumes surge to around five times more than normal. “Everyone was trying to buy the dip for the next push and trading was going both ways — it was pretty wild.” \n\nFrom Melbourne to Tokyo, market participants were scrambling to trade and make sense of swings in Japan’s embattled currency <https://fortune.com/asia/2024/04/17/japan-trade-deficit-third-straight-year/> during one of its most volatile days in recent memory. The yen dropped by more than 1% <https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2024-04-29/yen-slides-past-160-against-dollar-for-first-time-since-1990> in early trading, before U-turning to rally sharply by more than 2% by around 1 p.m. in Singapore.\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/asia/2024/04/29/japan-central-bank-intervene-currency-markets-yen-34-year-low/'}

As a result, the dollar is now worth more than 157 Japanese yen, compared to just 129 in January 2023 [2].

The weak yen has had both positive and negative effects on Japan's economy. On the one hand, it has made exports cheaper and more competitive, which has benefited industries such as tourism and car manufacturing [3]. On the other hand, it has made imports more expensive, which has hurt households and industries that rely on imported inputs [3]. Some firms are now warning that the yen might be too weak, sending input prices higher and worsening inflation in the country [4].

As a result, the dollar is now worth more than 157 Japanese yen, compared to just 129 in January 2023 [2].

On the one hand, it has made exports cheaper and more competitive, which has benefited industries such as tourism and car manufacturing [3]. On the other hand, it has made imports more expensive, which has hurt households and industries that rely on imported inputs [3]. Some firms are now warning that the yen might be too weak, sending input prices higher and worsening inflation in the country [4].
